      <p>
       <b>
        Easter Island
       </b>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_language" title="Rapa Nui language">
        Rapa Nui
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="rap" xml:lang="rap">
         Rapa Nui
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         Isla de Pascua
        </span>
       </i>
       ) is a
       <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">
        Chilean
       </a>
       island in the southeastern Pacific Ocean, at the southeasternmost point of the
       <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_Triangle" title="Polynesian Triangle">
        Polynesian Triangle
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">
        Oceania
       </a>
       . Easter Island is famous for its 887 extant monumental statues, called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Moai" title="Moai">
         moai
        </a>
       </i>
       , created by the early
       <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_people" title="Rapa Nui people">
        Rapa Nui people
       </a>
       . In 1995,
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       named Easter Island a
       <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">
        World Heritage Site
       </a>
       , with much of the island protected within
       <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_National_Park" title="Rapa Nui National Park">
        Rapa Nui National Park
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Polynesian people most likely settled on Easter Island sometime between 700 and 1100 AD and created a thriving and industrious culture as evidenced by the island's numerous enormous stone
       <i>
        moai
       </i>
       and other artifacts. However, human activity, the introduction of the
       <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_rat" title="Polynesian rat">
        Polynesian rat
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">
        overpopulation
       </a>
       led to gradual
       <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">
        deforestation
       </a>
       and extinction of natural resources which severely weakened the Rapa Nui civilization.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-terry_hunt_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-terry_hunt-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By the time of European arrival in 1722, the island's population had dropped to 2,000–3,000 from an estimated high of approximately 15,000 just a century earlier. European diseases and Peruvian
       <a href="/wiki/Slave_raiding" title="Slave raiding">
        slave raiding
       </a>
       in the 1860s further reduced the Rapa Nui population, to a low of only 111 inhabitants in 1877.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peiser_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-peiser-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Easter Island is one of the most remote inhabited islands in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The nearest inhabited land (around 50 residents in 2013) is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitcairn_Island" title="Pitcairn Island">
        Pitcairn Island
       </a>
       , 2,075 kilometres (1,289 mi) away;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the nearest town with a population over 500 is
       <a href="/wiki/Rikitea" title="Rikitea">
        Rikitea
       </a>
       , on the island of
       <a href="/wiki/Mangareva" title="Mangareva">
        Mangareva
       </a>
       , 2,606 km (1,619 mi) away; the nearest continental point lies in central Chile, 3,512 kilometres (2,182 mi) away.
      </p>
      <p>
       Easter Island is a special territory of
       <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">
        Chile
       </a>
       that was annexed in 1888. Administratively, it belongs to the
       <a href="/wiki/Valpara%C3%ADso_Region" title="Valparaíso Region">
        Valparaíso Region
       </a>
       , and, more specifically, it is the only
       <a href="/wiki/Communes_of_Chile" title="Communes of Chile">
        commune
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Chile" title="Provinces of Chile">
        Province
       </a>
       Isla de Pascua.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to the 2012 Chilean census, the island has about 5,800 residents, of whom some 60 percent are descendants of the aboriginal Rapa Nui.
      </p>
      <p>
       Easter Island is considered part of
       <a href="/wiki/Insular_Chile" title="Insular Chile">
        Insular Chile
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

          </span>

          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34">
         <a href="#References">
          <span class="tocnumber">

          </span>

        </li>

        </li>

      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Name">
        Name
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The name "Easter Island" was given by the island's first recorded European visitor, the Dutch explorer
       <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Roggeveen" title="Jacob Roggeveen">
        Jacob Roggeveen
       </a>
       , who encountered it on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Easter_Sunday" title="Easter Sunday">
        Easter Sunday
       </a>
       (5 April) in 1722, while searching for
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_Davis_(buccaneer)" title="Edward Davis (buccaneer)">
        Davis or David's island
       </a>
       . Roggeveen named it
       <i>
        Paasch-Eyland
       </i>
       (18th-century
       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">
        Dutch
       </a>
       for "Easter Island").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrjournal_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-jrjournal-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The island's official Spanish name,
       <i>
        Isla de Pascua
       </i>
       , also means "Easter Island".
      </p>
      <p>
       The current Polynesian name of the island,
       <i>
        Rapa Nui
       </i>
       ("Big Rapa"), was coined after the slave raids of the early 1860s, and refers to the island's topographic resemblance to the island of
       <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Iti" title="Rapa Iti">
        Rapa
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Bass_Islands_(French_Polynesia)" title="Bass Islands (French Polynesia)">
        Bass Islands
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Austral_Islands" title="Austral Islands">
        Austral Islands
       </a>
       group.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, Norwegian
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnographer" title="Ethnographer">
        ethnographer
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Thor_Heyerdahl" title="Thor Heyerdahl">
        Thor Heyerdahl
       </a>
       argued that
       <i>
        Rapa
       </i>
       was the original name of Easter Island and that
       <i>
        Rapa Iti
       </i>
       was named by refugees from there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The phrase
       <i>
        Te pito o te henua
       </i>
       has been said to be the original name of the island since French ethnologist
       <a href="/wiki/Alphonse_Pinart" title="Alphonse Pinart">
        Alphonse Pinart
       </a>
       gave it the romantic translation "the Navel of the World" in his
       <i>
        Voyage à l'Île de Pâques
       </i>
       , published in 1877.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/William_Churchill_(ethnologist)" title="William Churchill (ethnologist)">
        William Churchill
       </a>
       (1912) inquired about the phrase and was told that there were three
       <i>
        te pito o te henua
       </i>
       , these being the three capes (land's ends) of the island. The phrase appears to have been used in the same sense as the designation of "Land's End" at the tip of Cornwall. He was unable to elicit a Polynesian name for the island itself, and concluded that there may not have been one.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       According to Barthel (1974),
       <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">
        oral tradition
       </a>
       has it that the island was first named
       <i>
        Te pito o te kainga a Hau Maka
       </i>
       , "The little piece of land of Hau Maka".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, there are two words pronounced
       <i>
        pito
       </i>
       in Rapa Nui, one meaning 'end' and one 'navel', and the phrase can thus also mean "the Navel of the World". Another name,
       <i>
        Mata ki te rangi
       </i>
       , means "Eyes looking to the sky".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beck_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-beck-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Islanders are referred to in Spanish as
       <i>
        pascuense
       </i>
       ; however it is common to refer to members of the indigenous community as
       <i>
        Rapa Nui
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Easter_Island" title="History of Easter Island">
        History of Easter Island
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">
        Introduction
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Introduction">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Oral tradition states the island was first settled by a two canoe expedition, originating from Marae Renga (or Marae Toe Hau), and led by the chief Hotu Matu'a and his captain Tu'u ko Iho. The island was first scouted after Haumaka dreamed of such a far off country, Hotu deemed it a worthwhile place to flee from a neighboring chief, one to whom he had already lost three battles. At their time of arrival, the island had one lone settler, Nga Tavake 'a Te Rona. After a brief stay at Anakena, the colonists settled in different parts of the island. Hotu's heir, Tu'u ma Heke, was born on the island. Tu'u ko Iho is viewed as the leader who brought the statues and caused them to walk.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fischer-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The earliest radiocarbon dates for the island, AD 690±130, occur on the southwestern portion of the island at
       <a href="/wiki/Ahu_Tahai" title="Ahu Tahai">
        Ahu Tahai
       </a>
       near Hanga Roa. Archaeological and paleobotanical evidence also point to this area as the early center of habitation. Anthropometrical, skeletal, and linguistic evidence show the Easter Islanders to be close relatives of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mangareva" title="Mangareva">
        Mangarevans
       </a>
       . Thus, the Easter Islanders are considered to be South-East Polynesians, originating from the general area of Mangareva-Pitcairn-Henderson. Contact, at least culturally, carried onwards for at least 400 years. Similar sacred zones with statuary (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Marae" title="Marae">
         marae
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahu_(Easter_Island)" title="Ahu (Easter Island)">
        <i>
         ahu
        </i>
       </a>
       ) in East Polynesia lends support to this interaction from the 12th to the 15th centuries. The south coast was colonized starting in the 1300s. Then, Easter Island appears to have become isolated by about 1500, and mo'ai carving and transport ceased. The last forest was cut down by about 1640, and by about 1700, the flora was almost completely gone. The soil was eroded or leached, and water resources dried up. Only the three crater swamps, and isolated springs such as Vai Tapa Eru, provided fresh water. A peak population of about 12,000 seen in the 14th and 15th centuries had shrunk to perhaps 6,000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fischer-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         17-18,20-21,31,41-45
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By the 15th century, two confederations,
       <i>
        hanau
       </i>
       , of social groupings,
       <i>
        mata
       </i>
       , existed, based on lineage. The western and northern portion of the island belonged to the Tu'u, which included the royal Miru, with the royal center at Anakena, though Tahai and Te Peu served as earlier capitals. The eastern portion of the island belonged to the 'Otu 'Itu. Shortly after the Dutch visit, from 1724 until 1750, the 'Otu 'Itu fought the Tu'u for control of the island. This fighting would continue until the 1860s. Famine followed the burning of huts and the destruction of fields. Social control vanished as the ordered way of life gave way to lawlessness and predatory bands as the warrior class took over. Homelessness prevailed with many living underground. After the Spanish visit, from 1770 onwards, a period of statue toppling,
       <i>
        huri mo'ai
       </i>
       , commenced. This was an attempt by competing groups to destroy the socio-spiritual power, or
       <i>
        mana
       </i>
       , represented by statues, making sure to break them in the fall to ensure they were dead and without power. None were left standing by the time of the arrival of the French missionaries in the 1860s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_17-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fischer-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         21-24,27,54-56,64-65
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Between 1862 and 1888, about 94% of the population perished or emigrated. The island was victimized by
       <a href="/wiki/Blackbirding" title="Blackbirding">
        Blackbirding
       </a>
       from 1862 to 1863, resulting in the abduction or killing of about 1500, with 1408 working as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indentured_servant" title="Indentured servant">
        indentured servants
       </a>
       in Peru. Only about a dozen were to return to Easter Island, but they brought smallpox, which decimated the remaining population of 1500. Those who perished included the island's
       <i>
        tumu ivi 'atua
       </i>
       , bearers of the island's culture, history, and genealogy besides the
       <i>
        rongorongo
       </i>
       experts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_17-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fischer-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         86-91
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discussion">
        Discussion
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Discussion">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Estimated dates of initial settlement of Easter Island have ranged from 300 to 1200 AD, approximately coinciding with the arrival of the first settlers in Hawaii. Rectifications in
       <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">
        radiocarbon dating
       </a>
       have changed almost all of the previously posited early settlement dates in Polynesia. Rapa Nui is now considered to have been settled in the narrower range of 700 to 1100 AD. Ongoing archaeological studies suggest a still-later date: "Radiocarbon dates for the earliest stratigraphic layers at Anakena, Easter Island, and analysis of previous radiocarbon dates imply that the island was colonized late, about 1200 AD. Significant ecological impacts and major cultural investments in monumental architecture and statuary thus began soon after initial settlement."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huntlipo2006_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-huntlipo2006-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huntlipo2011_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-huntlipo2011-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       According to oral tradition, the first settlement was at
       <a href="/wiki/Anakena" title="Anakena">
        Anakena
       </a>
       . Researchers have noted that the Caleta Anakena landing point provides the island's best shelter from prevailing swells as well as a sandy beach for canoe landings and launchings so it appeals as a likely early place of settlement. However, this conclusion contradicts radiocarbon dating, according to which other sites preceded Anakena by many years, especially the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tahai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tahai (page does not exist)">
        Tahai
       </a>
       , whose radiocarbon dates precede Anakena's by several centuries.
      </p>
      <p>
       The island was most likely populated by Polynesians who navigated in canoes or
       <a href="/wiki/Catamaran" title="Catamaran">
        catamarans
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Gambier_Islands" title="Gambier Islands">
        Gambier Islands
       </a>
       (Mangareva, 2,600 km (1,600 mi) away) or the
       <a href="/wiki/Marquesas_Islands" title="Marquesas Islands">
        Marquesas Islands
       </a>
       , 3,200 km (2,000 mi) away. According to some theories, such as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynesian_Diaspora_Theory" title="Polynesian Diaspora Theory">
        Polynesian Diaspora Theory
       </a>
       , there is a possibility that early Polynesian settlers arrived from
       <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">
        South America
       </a>
       due to their remarkable sea-navigation abilities. Theorists have supported this through the agricultural evidence of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">
        sweet potato
       </a>
       . The sweet potato was a favoured crop found among Polynesian society for generations. However, the origins of the sweet potato trace back to
       <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">
        South America
       </a>
       , proving evidence of interaction at some point in time between these two geographic areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When
       <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">
        James Cook
       </a>
       visited the island, one of his crew members, a Polynesian from
       <a href="/wiki/Bora_Bora" title="Bora Bora">
        Bora Bora
       </a>
       , Hitihiti, was able to communicate with the Rapa Nui.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anne_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Anne-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         296–297
        </span>
       </sup>
       The language most similar to Rapa Nui is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mangarevan" title="Mangarevan">
        Mangarevan
       </a>
       , with an estimated 80 percent similarity in vocabulary. In 1999,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hokulea#1999_-_2000_Closing_the_Triangle" title="Hokulea">
        a voyage with reconstructed Polynesian boats
       </a>
       was able to reach Easter Island from Mangareva in 19 days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hodges_easter-island.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2500" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Hodges_easter-island.jpg/250px-Hodges_easter-island.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Hodges_easter-island.jpg/375px-Hodges_easter-island.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Hodges_easter-island.jpg/500px-Hodges_easter-island.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hodges_easter-island.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          A View of the Monuments of Easter Island, Rapanui
         </i>
         , c. 1775–1776 by
         <a href="/wiki/William_Hodges" title="William Hodges">
          William Hodges
         </a>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maritime_23-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-maritime-23">
           [23]
          </a>
         </sup>
         The earliest known painting of Easter Island.
         <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
          [
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
            <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">
             citation needed
            </span>
           </a>
          </i>
          ]
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       According to oral traditions recorded by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_(Christianity)" title="Mission (Christianity)">
        missionaries
       </a>
       in the 1860s, the island originally had a strong
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_system" title="Class system">
        class system
       </a>
       , with an
       <i>
        ariki
       </i>
       , or high
       <a href="/wiki/Tribal_chief" title="Tribal chief">
        chief
       </a>
       , wielding great power over nine other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clans" title="Clans">
        clans
       </a>
       and their respective chiefs. The high chief was the eldest descendent through first-born lines of the island's legendary founder,
       <a href="/wiki/Hotu_Matu%27a" title="Hotu Matu'a">
        Hotu Matu'a
       </a>
       . The most visible element in the culture was the production of massive statues called
       <a href="/wiki/Moai" title="Moai">
        moai
       </a>
       that some believe represented
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">
        deified ancestors
       </a>
       . According to
       <i>
        National Geographic
       </i>
       , "Most scholars suspect that the moai were created to honor ancestors, chiefs, or other important personages, However, no written and little oral history exists on the island, so it’s impossible to be certain."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It was believed that the living had a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbiotic_relationship" title="Symbiotic relationship">
        symbiotic relationship
       </a>
       with the dead in which the dead provided everything that the living needed (health, fertility of land and animals, fortune etc.) and the living, through offerings, provided the dead with a better place in the spirit world. Most settlements were located on the coast and most moai were erected along the coastline, watching over their descendants in the settlements before them, with their backs toward the spirit world in the sea.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Jared_Diamond" title="Jared Diamond">
        Jared Diamond
       </a>
       suggested that
       <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism" title="Cannibalism">
        cannibalism
       </a>
       took place on Easter Island after the construction of the moai contributed to
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">
        environmental
       </a>
       degradation when extreme deforestation destabilized an already precarious ecosystem.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Archeological record shows that at the time of the initial settlement the island was home to many species of trees, including at least three species which grew up to 15 metres (49 ft) or more:
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Paschalococos" title="Paschalococos">
         Paschalococos
        </a>
       </i>
       – possibly the largest palm trees in the world at the time,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Alphitonia" title="Alphitonia">
         Alphitonia zizyphoides
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Elaeocarpus" title="Elaeocarpus">
         Elaeocarpus rarotongensis
        </a>
       </i>
       , as well as at least six species of native land birds. A major factor that contributed to the extinction of multiple plant species was the introduction of the
       <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_rat" title="Polynesian rat">
        Polynesian rat
       </a>
       . Studies by paleobotanists have shown rats can dramatically affect the reproduction of vegetation in an ecosystem. In the case of Rapa Nui, recovered plant shell seeds showed markings of being gnawed on by rats.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-terry_hunt_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-terry_hunt-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Barbara A. West wrote, "Sometime before the arrival of Europeans on Easter Island, the Rapanui experienced a tremendous upheaval in their social system brought about by a change in their island's ecology... By the time of European arrival in 1722, the island's population had dropped to 2,000–3,000 from a high of approximately 15,000 just a century earlier."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By that time, 21 species of trees and all species of land birds became extinct through some combination of overharvesting/overhunting, rat predation, and climate change. The island was largely deforested, and it did not have any trees more than 3 metres (10 feet) tall. Loss of large trees meant that residents were no longer able to build seaworthy vessels, significantly diminishing their fishing abilities. One theory regarding the deforestation that caused such ecological and social damage was that the trees were used as rollers to move the statues to their place of erection from the quarry at
       <a href="/wiki/Rano_Raraku" title="Rano Raraku">
        Rano Raraku
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Deforestation also affected agricultural production on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui" title="Rapa Nui">
        Rapa Nui
       </a>
       . At first, the native tropical forests provided ideal shade cover for soil. But with many of the native forest being destroyed, the topsoil became eroded causing a sharp decline in agricultural production.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-terry_hunt_4-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-terry_hunt-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was further exacerbated by the loss of land birds and the collapse in seabird populations as a potential source of food. By the 18th century, residents of the island were largely sustained by farming, with domestic chickens as the primary source of protein.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As the island became overpopulated and resources diminished, warriors known as
       <i>
        matatoa
       </i>
       gained more power and the Ancestor Cult ended, making way for the Bird Man Cult. Beverly Haun wrote, "The concept of mana (power) invested in hereditary leaders was recast into the person of the birdman, apparently beginning circa 1540, and coinciding with the final vestiges of the moai period."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This cult maintained that, although the ancestors still provided for their descendants, the medium through which the living could contact the dead was no longer statues, but human beings chosen through a competition. The god responsible for creating humans,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Makemake_(mythology)" title="Makemake (mythology)">
        Makemake
       </a>
       , played an important role in this process.
       <a href="/wiki/Katherine_Routledge" title="Katherine Routledge">
        Katherine Routledge
       </a>
       , who systematically collected the island's traditions in her 1919 expedition,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-routledge_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-routledge-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       showed that the competitions for Bird Man (Rapanui:
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tangata_manu" title="Tangata manu">
         tangata manu
        </a>
       </i>
       ) started around 1760, after the arrival of the first Europeans, and ended in 1878, with the construction of the first church by Roman Catholic missionaries who formally arrived in 1864.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petroglyphs" title="Petroglyphs">
        Petroglyphs
       </a>
       representing Bird Men on Easter Island are exactly the same as some in Hawaii, indicating that this concept was probably brought by the original settlers; only the competition itself was unique to Easter Island.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rano-Kau-2b-Birdman-Cult.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="600" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Rano-Kau-2b-Birdman-Cult.JPG/250px-Rano-Kau-2b-Birdman-Cult.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Rano-Kau-2b-Birdman-Cult.JPG/375px-Rano-Kau-2b-Birdman-Cult.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Rano-Kau-2b-Birdman-Cult.JPG/500px-Rano-Kau-2b-Birdman-Cult.JPG 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rano-Kau-2b-Birdman-Cult.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Motu_Nui" title="Motu Nui">
          Motu Nui
         </a>
         islet, part of the Birdman Cult ceremony
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       According to Diamond and Heyerdahl's version of the island's history, the
       <i>
        huri mo'ai
       </i>
       —"statue-toppling"—continued into the 1830s as a part of fierce internal wars. By 1838 the only standing moai were on the slopes of
       <a href="/wiki/Rano_Raraku" title="Rano Raraku">
        Rano Raraku
       </a>
       , in
       <a href="/wiki/Hoa_Hakananai%27a" title="Hoa Hakananai'a">
        Hoa Hakananai'a
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Orongo" title="Orongo">
        Orongo
       </a>
       , and Ariki Paro in Ahu Te Pito Kura. A study headed by Douglas Owsley published in 1994 asserted that there is little archaeological evidence of pre-European
       <a href="/wiki/Societal_collapse" title="Societal collapse">
        societal collapse
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Bone_pathology" title="Bone pathology">
        Bone pathology
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osteometric" title="Osteometric">
        osteometric
       </a>
       data from islanders of that period clearly suggest few fatalities can be attributed directly to violence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="European_contact">
        European contact
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: European contact">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The first-recorded European contact with the island was on 5 April (
       <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">
        Easter Sunday
       </a>
       ), 1722, by Dutch navigator
       <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Roggeveen" title="Jacob Roggeveen">
        Jacob Roggeveen
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anne_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Anne-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His visit resulted in the death of about a dozen islanders, including the
       <i>
        tumu ivi 'atua
       </i>
       , and the wounding of many others.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_17-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fischer-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         46-53
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The next foreign visitors (on 15 November 1770) were two Spanish ships,
       <i>
        San Lorenzo
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Santa Rosalia
       </i>
       , under the command of Captain Don
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Felipe_Gonzalez_de_Ahedo" title="Felipe Gonzalez de Ahedo">
        Felipe Gonzalez de Ahedo
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anne_21-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Anne-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         238,504
        </span>
       </sup>
       The Spanish were amazed by the "standing idols", all of which were erect that you witnessed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_17-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fischer-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         60-64
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Four years later, in 1774, British explorer
       <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">
        James Cook
       </a>
       visited Easter Island; he reported that some statues had been toppled. Through the interpretation of Hitihiti, Cook learned the statues commemorated their former high chiefs, including their names and ranks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anne_21-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Anne-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         296–297
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The British ship
       <a href="/wiki/HMS_Blossom_(1806)" title="HMS Blossom (1806)">
        HMS
        <i>
         Blossom
        </i>
       </a>
       arrived in 1825.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_17-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fischer-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         79
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">
        19th century
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 19th century">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A series of devastating events killed or removed most of the population in the 1860s. In December 1862,
       <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">
        Peruvian
       </a>
       slave raiders struck. Violent abductions continued for several months, eventually capturing around 1,500 men and women, half of the island's population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Among those captured were the island's paramount chief, his heir, and those who knew how to read and write the
       <a href="/wiki/Rongorongo" title="Rongorongo">
        rongorongo
       </a>
       script, the only Polynesian script to have been found to date. (Although serious debate exists about whether this is
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-writing" title="Proto-writing">
        proto-writing
       </a>
       or true writing.)
      </p>
      <p>
       When the slave raiders were forced to repatriate the people they had kidnapped, carriers of
       <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">
        smallpox
       </a>
       disembarked together with a few survivors on each of the islands.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This created devastating epidemics from Easter Island to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marquesas" title="Marquesas">
        Marquesas
       </a>
       islands. Easter Island's population was reduced to the point where some of the dead were not even buried.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_17-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fischer-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         91
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">
        Tuberculosis
       </a>
       , introduced by whalers in the mid-19th century, had already killed several islanders when the first Christian missionary,
       <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Eyraud" title="Eugène Eyraud">
        Eugène Eyraud
       </a>
       , died from this disease in 1867. About a quarter of the island's population succumbed along with him. In the following years, the managers of the sheep ranch and the missionaries started buying the newly available lands of the deceased, and this led to great confrontations between natives and settlers.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Queen_of_Easter_Island_meets_Pinart_in_1877.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1492" data-file-width="2040" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Queen_of_Easter_Island_meets_Pinart_in_1877.jpg/300px-Queen_of_Easter_Island_meets_Pinart_in_1877.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Queen_of_Easter_Island_meets_Pinart_in_1877.jpg/450px-Queen_of_Easter_Island_meets_Pinart_in_1877.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Queen_of_Easter_Island_meets_Pinart_in_1877.jpg/600px-Queen_of_Easter_Island_meets_Pinart_in_1877.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Queen_of_Easter_Island_meets_Pinart_in_1877.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         "Queen Mother" Koreto with her daughters "Queen" Caroline and Harriette in 1877
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Dutrou-Bornier" title="Jean-Baptiste Dutrou-Bornier">
        Jean-Baptiste Dutrou-Bornier
       </a>
       bought up all of the island apart from the missionaries' area around
       <a href="/wiki/Hanga_Roa" title="Hanga Roa">
        Hanga Roa
       </a>
       and moved a few hundred Rapanui to
       <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">
        Tahiti
       </a>
       to work for his backers. In 1871 the missionaries, having fallen out with Dutrou-Bornier, evacuated all but 171 Rapanui to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gambier_islands" title="Gambier islands">
        Gambier islands
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Those who remained were mostly older men. Six years later, only 111 people lived on Easter Island, and only 36 of them had any offspring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From that point on the island's population slowly recovered. But with over 97% of the population dead or gone in less than a decade, much of the island's cultural knowledge had been lost.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Salmon,_Jr." title="Alexander Salmon, Jr.">
        Alexander Salmon, Jr.
       </a>
       , a son of an English Jewish merchant and a
       <a href="/wiki/P%C5%8Dmare_Dynasty" title="Pōmare Dynasty">
        Pōmare Dynasty
       </a>
       princess, eventually worked to repatriate workers from his inherited copra plantation. He eventually bought up all lands on the island with the exception of the mission, and was its sole employer. He worked to develop tourism on the island, and was the principal informant for the British and German archaeological expeditions for the island. He sent several pieces of genuine
       <a href="/wiki/Rongorongo" title="Rongorongo">
        Rongorongo
       </a>
       to his niece's husband, the German consul in Valparaíso, Chile. Salmon sold the Brander Easter Island holdings to the Chilean government on January 2 1888 and signed as a witness to the cession of the island. He returned to Tahiti in December of that year. He effectively ruled the island from 1878 until his cession to Chile in 1888.
      </p>
      <p>
       Easter Island was annexed by Chile on 9 September 1888 by
       <a href="/wiki/Policarpo_Toro" title="Policarpo Toro">
        Policarpo Toro
       </a>
       by means of the "Treaty of Annexation of the Island" (Tratado de Anexión de la isla). Toro, then representing the government of Chile, signed with
       <a href="/wiki/Atamu_Tekena" title="Atamu Tekena">
        Atamu Tekena
       </a>
       , designated "King" by the Roman Catholic missionaries after the paramount chief and his heir had died. The validity of this treaty is still contested by some Rapanui. Officially, Chile purchased the nearly all encompassing Mason-Brander sheep ranch, comprised from lands purchased from the descendants of Rapanui who died during the epidemics, and then claimed sovereignty over the island.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">
        20th century
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 20th century">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Until the 1960s the surviving Rapanui were confined to
       <a href="/wiki/Hanga_Roa" title="Hanga Roa">
        Hanga Roa
       </a>
       . The rest of the island was rented to the
       <a href="/wiki/Williamson-Balfour_Company" title="Williamson-Balfour Company">
        Williamson-Balfour Company
       </a>
       as a sheep farm until 1953.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The island was then managed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Chilean_Navy" title="Chilean Navy">
        Chilean Navy
       </a>
       until 1966, at which point the island was reopened in its entirety. In 1966 the Rapanui were given Chilean citizenship.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:General_Pinochet_junto_a_una_pascuense.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1384" data-file-width="1976" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/General_Pinochet_junto_a_una_pascuense.jpg/200px-General_Pinochet_junto_a_una_pascuense.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/General_Pinochet_junto_a_una_pascuense.jpg/300px-General_Pinochet_junto_a_una_pascuense.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/General_Pinochet_junto_a_una_pascuense.jpg/400px-General_Pinochet_junto_a_una_pascuense.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:General_Pinochet_junto_a_una_pascuense.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         General Pinochet posing with a native
         <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_people" title="Rapa Nui people">
          Rapa Nui
         </a>
         woman
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Following the
       <a href="/wiki/1973_Chilean_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1973 Chilean coup d'état">
        1973 Chilean coup d'état
       </a>
       that brought
       <a href="/wiki/Augusto_Pinochet" title="Augusto Pinochet">
        Augusto Pinochet
       </a>
       to power, Easter Island was placed under
       <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">
        martial law
       </a>
       . Tourism slowed down and private property was restored. During his time in power, Pinochet visited Easter Island on three occasions. The military built a number of new military facilities and a new city hall.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After an agreement in 1985 between Chile and United States, the runway at
       <a href="/wiki/Mataveri_International_Airport" title="Mataveri International Airport">
        Mataveri International Airport
       </a>
       was enlarged and was inaugurated in 1987. The runway was expanded 423 metres (1,388 ft) reaching 3,353 metres (11,001 ft). Pinochet is reported to have refused to attend the inauguration in protest of pressures from the United States to attend human rights cases.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">
        21st century
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 21st century">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Fishers of Rapa Nui have shown their concern of illegal fishing on the island. “Since the year 2000 we started to lose tuna, which is the basis of the fishing on the island, so then we began to take the fish from the shore to feed our families, but in less than two years we depleted all of it,” Pakarati said.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 30 July 2007, a constitutional reform gave Easter Island and the
       <a href="/wiki/Juan_Fern%C3%A1ndez_Islands" title="Juan Fernández Islands">
        Juan Fernández Islands
       </a>
       (also known as
       <a href="/wiki/Robinson_Crusoe_Island" title="Robinson Crusoe Island">
        Robinson Crusoe Island
       </a>
       ) the status of "special territories" of Chile. Pending the enactment of a special charter, the island continued to be governed as a province of the V Region of
       <a href="/wiki/Valpara%C3%ADso" title="Valparaíso">
        Valparaíso
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_July_11,_2010" title="Solar eclipse of July 11, 2010">
        total solar eclipse
       </a>
       visible from Easter Island occurred for the first time in over 1,300 years on 11 July 2010, at 18:15:15.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Species of fish were collected in Easter Island for one month in different habitats including shallow lava pools, depths of 43 meters, and deep waters. Within these habitats, two holotypes and paratypes,
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antennarius_randalli" title="Antennarius randalli">
         Antennarius randalli
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Antennarius_moai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Antennarius moai (page does not exist)">
         Antennarius moai
        </a>
       </i>
       , were discovered. These are considered frog-fish because of their characteristics: "12 dorsal rays, last two or three branched; bony part of first dorsal spine slightly shorter than second dorsal spine; body without bold zebra-like markings; caudal peduncle short, but distinct; last pelvic ray divided; pectoral rays 11 or 12".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_rights_movement">
        Indigenous rights movement
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Indigenous rights movement">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Starting in August 2010, members of the indigenous Hitorangi clan occupied the Hangaroa Eco Village and Spa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The occupiers allege that the hotel was bought from the Pinochet government, in violation of a Chilean agreement with the indigenous Rapa Nui, in the 1990s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The occupiers say their ancestors had been cheated into giving up the land.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to a
       <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">
        BBC
       </a>
       report, on 3 December 2010, at least 25 people were injured when Chilean police using pellet guns attempted to evict from these buildings a group of Rapa Nui who had claimed that the land the buildings stood on had been illegally taken from their ancestors.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In January 2011, the UN's Special Rapporteur on Indigenous People, James Anaya, expressed concern about the treatment of the indigenous Rapa Nui by the Chilean government, urging Chile to "make every effort to conduct a dialogue in good faith with representatives of the
       <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_people" title="Rapa Nui people">
        Rapa Nui people
       </a>
       to solve, as soon as possible the real underlying problems that explain the current situation".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_44-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The incident ended in February 2011, when up to 50 armed police broke into the hotel to remove the final five occupiers. They were arrested by the government and no injuries were reported.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_44-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Easter_island_and_south_america.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="345" data-file-width="345" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Easter_island_and_south_america.jpg/220px-Easter_island_and_south_america.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Easter_island_and_south_america.jpg/330px-Easter_island_and_south_america.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Easter_island_and_south_america.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Easter_island_and_south_america.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Easter Island,
         <i>
          Salas y Gómez Islands
         </i>
         , South America and the islands in between
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Easter Island is one of the world's most isolated inhabited islands. Its closest inhabited neighbour is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitcairn_Island" title="Pitcairn Island">
        Pitcairn Island
       </a>
       , 2,075 km (1,289 mi) to the west, with fewer than 100 inhabitants. The nearest continental point lies in central Chile near
       <a href="/wiki/Concepci%C3%B3n,_Chile" title="Concepción, Chile">
        Concepción
       </a>
       , at 3,512 kilometres (2,182 mi). Easter Island's
       <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">
        latitude
       </a>
       is similar to that of
       <a href="/wiki/Caldera,_Chile" title="Caldera, Chile">
        Caldera, Chile
       </a>
       , and it lies 3,510 km (2,180 mi) west of continental Chile at its nearest point (between
       <a href="/wiki/Lota,_Chile" title="Lota, Chile">
        Lota
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lebu,_Chile" title="Lebu, Chile">
        Lebu
       </a>
       in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biob%C3%ADo_Region" title="Biobío Region">
        Biobío Region
       </a>
       ).
       <a href="/wiki/Isla_Salas_y_G%C3%B3mez" title="Isla Salas y Gómez">
        Isla Salas y Gómez
       </a>
       , 415 km (258 mi) to the east, is closer but is uninhabited. Archipelago
       <a href="/wiki/Tristan_da_Cunha" title="Tristan da Cunha">
        Tristan da Cunha
       </a>
       in the southern Atlantic competes for the title of the most remote island, lying 2,430 kilometres (1,510 mi) from
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">
        Saint Helena
       </a>
       island and 2,816 kilometres (1,750 mi) from the
       <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">
        South African
       </a>
       coast.
      </p>
      <p>
       The island is about 24.6 km (15.3 mi) long by 12.3 km (7.6 mi) at its widest point; its overall shape is triangular. It has an area of 163.6 square kilometres (63.2 sq mi), and a maximum altitude of 507 metres (1,663 ft). There are three
       <i>
        Rano
       </i>
       (freshwater
       <a href="/wiki/Crater_lake" title="Crater lake">
        crater lakes
       </a>
       ), at
       <a href="/wiki/Rano_Kau" title="Rano Kau">
        Rano Kau
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rano_Raraku" title="Rano Raraku">
        Rano Raraku
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rano_Aroi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rano Aroi (page does not exist)">
        Rano Aroi
       </a>
       , near the summit of Terevaka, but no permanent streams or rivers.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">
        Geology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rapa-Nui-Landscape.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Rapa-Nui-Landscape.jpg/250px-Rapa-Nui-Landscape.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Rapa-Nui-Landscape.jpg/375px-Rapa-Nui-Landscape.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Rapa-Nui-Landscape.jpg/500px-Rapa-Nui-Landscape.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rapa-Nui-Landscape.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Typical landscape on Easter Island; rounded extinct volcanoes covered in low vegetation.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Easter Island is a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic" title="Volcanic">
        volcanic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/High_island" title="High island">
        high island
       </a>
       , consisting mainly of three extinct coalesced
       <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">
        volcanoes
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Terevaka" title="Terevaka">
        Terevaka
       </a>
       (altitude 507 metres) forms the bulk of the island, while two other volcanoes,
       <a href="/wiki/Poike" title="Poike">
        Poike
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rano_Kau" title="Rano Kau">
        Rano Kau
       </a>
       , form the eastern and southern headlands and give the island its roughly triangular shape. Lesser cones and other volcanic features include the crater
       <a href="/wiki/Rano_Raraku" title="Rano Raraku">
        Rano Raraku
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Cinder_cone" title="Cinder cone">
        cinder cone
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Puna_Pau" title="Puna Pau">
        Puna Pau
       </a>
       and many volcanic caves including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lava_tubes" title="Lava tubes">
        lava tubes
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gvp_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gvp-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Poike used to be a separate island until volcanic material from Terevaka united it to the larger whole. The island is dominated by
       <a href="/wiki/Hawaiite" title="Hawaiite">
        hawaiite
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">
        basalt
       </a>
       flows which are rich in iron and show affinity with
       <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">
        igneous rocks
       </a>
       found in the
       <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_Islands" title="Galápagos Islands">
        Galápagos Islands
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Easter Island and surrounding islets, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Motu_Nui" title="Motu Nui">
        Motu Nui
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Motu_Iti_(Rapa_Nui)" title="Motu Iti (Rapa Nui)">
        Motu Iti
       </a>
       , form the summit of a large volcanic mountain rising over 2,000 metres (6,600 ft) from the sea bed. The mountain is part of the Sala y Gómez Ridge, a (mostly submarine)
       <a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">
        mountain range
       </a>
       with dozens of
       <a href="/wiki/Seamount" title="Seamount">
        seamounts
       </a>
       , formed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Easter_hotspot" title="Easter hotspot">
        Easter hotspot
       </a>
       . The range begins with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pukao_(Seamount)" title="Pukao (Seamount)">
        Pukao
       </a>
       and next
       <a href="/wiki/Moai_(seamount)" title="Moai (seamount)">
        Moai
       </a>
       , two seamounts to the west of Easter Island, and extends 2,700 km (1,700 mi) east to the
       <a href="/wiki/Nazca_Ridge" title="Nazca Ridge">
        Nazca Ridge
       </a>
       . The ridge was formed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Nazca_Plate" title="Nazca Plate">
        Nazca Plate
       </a>
       moving over the
       <a href="/wiki/Easter_hotspot" title="Easter hotspot">
        Easter hotspot
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-petro1_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-petro1-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Located about 350 km east of the
       <a href="/wiki/East_Pacific_Rise" title="East Pacific Rise">
        East Pacific Rise
       </a>
       , Easter Island lies within the Nazca Plate, bordering the
       <a href="/wiki/Easter_Plate" title="Easter Plate">
        Easter Microplate
       </a>
       . The Nazca-Pacific relative plate movement due to the
       <a href="/wiki/Seafloor_spreading" title="Seafloor spreading">
        seafloor spreading
       </a>
       , amounts to about 150
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milimetre" title="Milimetre">
        mm
       </a>
       per year. This movement over the Easter hotspot has resulted in the Easter Seamount Chain, which merges into the Nazca Ridge further to the east. Easter Island and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sala_y_G%C3%B3mez" title="Sala y Gómez">
        Sala y Gómez
       </a>
       are surface representations of that chain. The chain has progressively younger ages to the west. The current hotspot location is speculated to be west of Easter Island, amidst the Ahu, Umu and Tupa submarine volcanic fields and the Pukao and Moai seamounts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vezzoli_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vezzoli-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Easter Island lies atop the Rano Kau Ridge, and consists of three
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shield_volcanoes" title="Shield volcanoes">
        shield volcanoes
       </a>
       with parallel geologic histories. Poike and Rano Kau exist on the east and south slopes of Terevaka, respectively. Rano Kau developed between 0.78 and 0.46
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megaannum" title="Megaannum">
        Ma
       </a>
       from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tholeiitic" title="Tholeiitic">
        tholeiitic
       </a>
       to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calc-alkaline" title="Calc-alkaline">
        alkalic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">
        basalts
       </a>
       . This volcano possesses a clearly defined summit
       <a href="/wiki/Caldera" title="Caldera">
        caldera
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Benmoreite" title="Benmoreite">
        Benmoreitic
       </a>
       lavas
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrusive_(geology)" title="Extrusive (geology)">
        extruded
       </a>
       about the rim from 0.35 to 0.34 Ma. Finally, between 0.24 and 0.11 Ma, a 6.5 km
       <a href="/wiki/Fracture_(geology)" title="Fracture (geology)">
        fissure
       </a>
       developed along a NE-SW trend, forming
       <a href="/wiki/Monogenetic_volcanic_field" title="Monogenetic volcanic field">
        monogenetic
       </a>
       vents and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhyolitic" title="Rhyolitic">
        rhyolitic
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intrusion" title="Intrusion">
        intrusions
       </a>
       . These include the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryptodome" title="Cryptodome">
        cryptodome
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Islet" title="Islet">
        islets
       </a>
       of Motu Nui and Motu Iti, the islet of Motu Kao Kao, the
       <a href="/wiki/Sheet_intrusion" title="Sheet intrusion">
        sheet intrusion
       </a>
       of Te Kari Kari, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perlitic_Structure" title="Perlitic Structure">
        perlitic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">
        obsidian
       </a>
       Te Manavai dome and the Maunga Orito dome.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vezzoli_52-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vezzoli-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Poike formed from tholeiitic to alkali basalts from 0.78 to 0.41 Ma. Its summit collapsed into a caldera which was subsequently filled by the Puakatiki
       <a href="/wiki/Lava_cone" title="Lava cone">
        lava cone
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pahoehoe" title="Pahoehoe">
        pahoehoe
       </a>
       flows at 0.36 Ma. Finally, the
       <a href="/wiki/Trachyte" title="Trachyte">
        trachytic
       </a>
       lava domes of Maunga Vai a Heva, Maunga Tea Tea, and Maunga Parehe formed along a NE-SW trending fissure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vezzoli_52-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vezzoli-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Terevaka formed around 0.77 Ma of tholeiitic to alkali basalts, followed by the collapse of its summit into a caldera. Then at about 0.3Ma, cinder cones formed along a NNE-SSW trend on the western rim, while
       <a href="/wiki/Porphyritic" title="Porphyritic">
        porphyritic
       </a>
       benmoreitic lava filled the caldera, and pahoehoe flowed towards the northern coast, forming lava tubes, and to the southeast. Lava domes and a vent complex formed in the Maunga Puka area, while breccias formed along the vents on the western portion of Rano Aroi crater. This volcano's southern and southeastern flanks are composed of younger flows consisting of basalt, alkali basalt, hawaiite,
       <a href="/wiki/Mugearite" title="Mugearite">
        mugearite
       </a>
       , and benmoreite from eruptive fissures starting at 0.24 Ma. The youngest lava flow, Roiho, is dated at 0.11 Ma. The Hanga O Teo embayment is interpreted to be a 200 m high landslide
       <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">
        scarp
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vezzoli_52-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vezzoli-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Rano Raraku and Maunga Toa Toa are isolated
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuff_cone" title="Tuff cone">
        tuff cones
       </a>
       of about 0.21 Ma. The crater of Rano Raraku contains a freshwater lake. The
       <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">
        stratified
       </a>
       tuff is composed of
       <a href="/wiki/Sideromelane" title="Sideromelane">
        sideromelane
       </a>
       , slightly altered to
       <a href="/wiki/Palagonite" title="Palagonite">
        palagonite
       </a>
       , and somewhat
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithified" title="Lithified">
        lithified
       </a>
       . The tuff contains
       <a href="/wiki/Lithic_fragment_(geology)" title="Lithic fragment (geology)">
        lithic fragments
       </a>
       of older lava flows. The northwest sector of Rano Raraku contains reddish
       <a href="/wiki/Ash" title="Ash">
        ash
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vezzoli_52-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vezzoli-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to Bandy, "...all of the great images of Easter Island are carved from" the light and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porous" title="Porous">
        porous
       </a>
       tuff from Rano Raraku. A carving was abandoned when a large, dense and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardness_scale" title="Hardness scale">
        hard
       </a>
       lithic fragment was encountered. However, these lithics became the basis for stone hammers and chisels. The Puna Pau crater contains an extremely porous
       <a href="/wiki/Pumice" title="Pumice">
        pumice
       </a>
       , from which was carved the Pukao "hats". The Maunga Orito obsidian was used to make the "mataa" spearheads.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the first half of the 20th century, steam reportedly came out of the Rano Kau crater wall. This was photographed by the island's manager, Mr. Edmunds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">
        Climate
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Easter_Island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Climate">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Chile" title="Climate of Chile">
        Climate of Chile
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Under the
       <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">
        Köppen climate classification
       </a>
       , the climate of Easter Island is classified as a
       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest_climate" title="Tropical rainforest climate">
        tropical rainforest climate
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Af
       </i>
       ) that borders on a
       <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">
        humid subtropical climate
       </a>
       . The lowest temperatures are recorded in July and August (minimum 15 °C or 59 °F) and the highest in February (maximum temperature 28 °C or 82 °F
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), the summer season in the southern hemisphere. Winters are relatively mild. The rainiest month is May, though the island experiences year-round rainfall.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Easter Island's isolated location exposes it to winds which help to keep the temperature fairly cool. Precipitation averages 1,118 millimetres or 44 inches per year. Occasionally, heavy rainfall and rainstorms strike the island. These occur mostly in the winter months (June–August). Since it is close to the
       <a href="/wiki/South_Pacific_High" title="South Pacific High">
        South Pacific High
       </a>
       and outside the range of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intertropical_convergence_zone" title="Intertropical convergence zone">
        intertropical convergence zone
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">
        cyclones
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane" title="Hurricane">
        hurricanes
       </a>
       do not occur around Easter island.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is significant temperature moderation due to its isolated position in the middle of the ocean.
      </p>
